881 <section id="_1">

882

883 <h2>

884General Information About Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, Fallopian Tube Cancer (FTC), and Primary Peritoneal Cancer (PPC)</h2>

885

886 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

887<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

888<h6>

889In This Section</h6>

890<ul>

891<li>

892<a href="#_416_toc">

893Incidence and Mortality</a>

894</li>

895<li>

896<a href="#_674_toc">

897Anatomy</a>

898</li>

899<li>

900<a href="#_557_toc">

901Risk Factors<ul>

902<li>

903<a href="#_822_toc">

904Family history and genetic alterations</a>

905</li>

906</ul>

907</a>

908</li>

909<li>

910<a href="#_560_toc">

911Clinical Presentation</a>

912</li>

913<li>

914<a href="#_676_toc">

915Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</a>

916</li>

917<li>

918<a href="#_561_toc">

919Prognostic Factors</a>

920</li>

921<li>

922<a href="#_708_toc">

923Follow-up</a>

924</li>

925</ul>

926</nav>

927<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

928<a href="/types/ovarian/patient/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq">

929Go to Patient Version</a>

930</div>

931<p id="_621" tabindex="-1">

932This PDQ summary addresses the staging and treatment of ovarian epithelial cancer, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC).</p>

933<p id="_622" tabindex="-1">

934Regardless of the site of origin, the hallmark of these cancers is their early peritoneal spread of metastases. The inclusion of FTC and PPC within the ovarian epithelial cancer designation is generally accepted because of much evidence that points to a common M&#252;llerian epithelium derivation and similar management of these three neoplasms. The hypothesis that many high-grade serous ovarian cancers (the most common histological subtype) may arise from precursor lesions that originate in the fimbriae of the fallopian tubes has been supported by findings from risk-reducing surgeries in healthy women with <em class="gene-name">

935BRCA1</em>

936 or <em class="gene-name">

937BRCA2</em>

938 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9391</a>

940] In addition, histologically similar cancers diagnosed as primary peritoneal carcinomas share molecular findings, such as loss or inactivation of the tumor-suppressor p53 and <em class="gene-name">

941BRCA1</em>

942 or <em class="gene-name">

943BRCA2</em>

944 proteins.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9452</a>

946] Therefore, high-grade serous adenocarcinomas arising from the fallopian tube and elsewhere in the peritoneal cavity, together with most ovarian epithelial cancers, represent extrauterine adenocarcinomas of M&#252;llerian epithelial origin and are staged and treated similarly to ovarian cancer. Since 2000, FTC and PPC have usually been included in ovarian cancer clinical trials.[<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9473</a>

948] </p>

949<p id="_820" tabindex="-1">

950Clear cell and endometrioid ovarian cancers that are linked to endometriosis have different gene-expression signatures, as do mucinous subtypes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9512</a>

952]</p>

953<p id="_623" tabindex="-1">

954Stromal and germ cell tumors are relatively uncommon and comprise fewer than 10% of cases. For more information, see <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-germ-cell-treatment-pdq">

955Ovarian Germ Cell Tumors Treatment</a>

956 and <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-low-malignant-treatment-pdq">

957Ovarian Low Malignant Potential Tumors Treatment</a>

958.</p>

959<section id="_416">

960<h3 id="_416_toc">

961Incidence and Mortality</h3>

962<p id="_867" tabindex="-1">

963Epithelial carcinoma of the ovary is one of the most common gynecologic malignancies, with almost 50% of all cases occurring in women older than 65 years. It is the fifth most frequent cause of cancer death in women.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

9644</a>

965,<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9665</a>

967]</p>

968<p id="_474" tabindex="-1">

969Estimated new cases and deaths from ovarian cancer in the United States in 2023:[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

9705</a>

971]</p>

972<div class="pdq-content-list">

973<ul id="_187">

974<li>

975New cases: 19,710.</li>

976<li>

977Deaths: 13,270.</li>

978</ul>

979</div>

980</section>

981<section id="_674">

982<h3 id="_674_toc">

983Anatomy</h3>

984<p id="_1001" tabindex="-1">

985The fimbriated ends of the fallopian tubes are in close apposition to the ovaries and in the peritoneal space, as opposed to the corpus uteri (body of the uterus) that is located under a layer of peritoneum.</p>

986<figure id="figure_946" class="image-center-medium">

987<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/609921.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

988Enlarge</a>

989<img id="_946" alt="Anatomy of the female reproductive system; drawing shows the uterus, myometrium (muscular outer layer of the uterus), endometrium (inner lining of the uterus), ovaries, fallopian tubes, cervix, and vagina." title="Anatomy of the female reproductive system; drawing shows the uterus, myometrium (muscular outer layer of the uterus), endometrium (inner lining of the uterus), ovaries, fallopian tubes, cervix, and vagina." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/609921-750.jpg">

990<figcaption class="caption-container">

991Normal female reproductive system anatomy.</figcaption>

992</figure>

993</section>

994<section id="_557">

995<h3 id="_557_toc">

996Risk Factors</h3>

997<section id="_sm_CDR0000753296_1">

998<p id="_sm_CDR0000753296_2" tabindex="-1">

999Increasing age is the most important risk factor for most cancers. Other risk factors for ovarian (epithelial) cancer include the following:</p>

1000<div class="pdq-content-list">

1001<ul id="_sm_CDR0000753296_3">

1002<li>

1003Family history of ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10046</a>

1005-<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

10068</a>

1007]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1008<ul id="_sm_CDR0000753296_4" class="list-dash">

1009<li>

1010 A first-degree relative (e.g., mother, daughter, or sister) with the disease.</li>

1011</ul>

1012</div>

1013</li>

1014<li>

1015Inherited risk.[<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

10169</a>

1017]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1018<ul id="_sm_CDR0000753296_5" class="list-dash">

1019<li>

1020 <em class="gene-name">

1021BRCA1</em>

1022 or <em class="gene-name">

1023BRCA2</em>

1024 gene mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10256</a>

1026,<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

102710</a>

1028]</li>

1029</ul>

1030</div>

1031</li>

1032<li>

1033Other hereditary conditions such as hereditary nonpolyposis colorectal cancer (HNPCC; also called Lynch syndrome).[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

10346</a>

1035,<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

10369</a>

1037]</li>

1038<li>

1039Endometriosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

104011</a>

1041-<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

104213</a>

1043]</li>

1044<li>

1045Hormone therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_1.14">

104614</a>

1047,<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

104815</a>

1049]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1050<ul id="_sm_CDR0000753296_9" class="list-dash">

1051<li>

1052Postmenopausal hormone replacement therapy.</li>

1053</ul>

1054</div>

1055</li>

1056<li>

1057Obesity.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

105816</a>

1059-<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

106018</a>

1061]<div class="pdq-content-list">

1062<ul id="_sm_CDR0000753296_6" class="list-dash">

1063<li>

1064 High body mass index.</li>

1065</ul>

1066</div>

1067</li>

1068<li>

1069Tall Height.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

107016</a>

1071-<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

107218</a>

1073]</li>

1074</ul>

1075</div>

1076</section>

1077<section id="_822">

1078<h4 id="_822_toc">

1079Family history and genetic alterations</h4>

1080<p id="_868" tabindex="-1">

1081The most

1082important risk factor for ovarian cancer is a history of ovarian cancer in a first-degree

1083relative (mother, daughter, or sister). Approximately 20% of ovarian cancers are familial, and although most of these are linked to mutations in either the <em class="gene-name">

1084BRCA1</em>

1085 or <em class="gene-name">

1086BRCA2</em>

1087 gene, several other genes have been implicated.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

108819</a>

1089,<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

109020</a>

1091] The risk is highest in women who have two or more first-degree relatives with ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

109221</a>

1093]

1094The risk is somewhat less for women who have one first-degree relative and one second-degree relative (grandmother or aunt) with ovarian cancer.</p>

1095<p id="_869" tabindex="-1">

1096In most families affected

1097with breast and ovarian cancer syndrome or site-specific ovarian cancer,

1098genetic linkage to the <em class="gene-name">

1099BRCA1</em>

1100 locus on chromosome 17q21 has been identified.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

110122</a>

1102-<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

110324</a>

1104]

1105<em class="gene-name">

1106BRCA2</em>

1107, also responsible for some instances of inherited ovarian and breast

1108cancer, has been mapped by genetic linkage to chromosome 13q12.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

110925</a>

1110] </p>

1111<p id="_823" tabindex="-1">

1112The

1113lifetime risk for developing ovarian cancer in patients harboring germline

1114mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1115BRCA1</em>

1116 is substantially increased over that of the general population.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

111726</a>

1118,<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

111927</a>

1120]

1121Two retrospective studies of patients with germline mutations in <em class="gene-name">

1122BRCA1</em>

1123 suggest

1124that the women in these studies have improved survival compared with <em class="gene-name">

1125BRCA1</em>

1126 mutation&#8211;negative

1127women.[<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

112828</a>

1129,<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

113029</a>

1131][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1132Level of evidence C1</a>

1133] Most women with a <em class="gene-name">

1134BRCA1</em>

1135 mutation probably have

1136family members with a history of ovarian and/or breast cancer; therefore,

1137the women in these studies may have been more vigilant and inclined to participate in cancer

1138screening programs that may have led to earlier detection.</p>

1139<p id="_715" tabindex="-1">

1140For women at

1141increased risk, prophylactic oophorectomy may be considered after age 35

1142 years if childbearing is complete. In a family-based study among 551 women with <em class="gene-name">

1143BRCA1</em>

1144 or <em class="gene-name">

1145BRCA2</em>

1146 mutations, of the 259 women who had undergone bilateral prophylactic oophorectomy, 2 (0.8%) developed subsequent papillary serous peritoneal carcinoma, and 6 (2.8%) had stage I ovarian cancer at the time of surgery. Of the 292 matched controls, 20% who did not have prophylactic surgery developed ovarian cancer. Prophylactic surgery was associated with a reduction in the risk of ovarian cancer that exceeded 90% (relative risk, 0.04; 95% confidence interval, 0.01&#8211;0.16), with an average follow-up of 9 years;[<a href="#cit/section_1.30">

114730</a>

1148] however, family-based studies may be associated with biases resulting from case selection and other factors that influence the estimate of benefit.[<a href="#cit/section_1.31">

114931</a>

1150] After

1151a prophylactic oophorectomy, a small percentage of women may develop a

1152primary peritoneal carcinoma that is similar in appearance to ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.32">

115332</a>

1154] </p>

1155<p id="_824" tabindex="-1">

1156For more information, see <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-prevention-pdq">

1157Ovarian, Fallopian Tube, and Primary Peritoneal Cancers Prevention</a>

1158 and <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/brca-genes-hp-pdq">

1159BRCA1 and BRCA2: Cancer Risks and Management</a>

1160.</p>

1161</section>

1162</section>

1163<section id="_560">

1164<h3 id="_560_toc">

1165Clinical Presentation</h3>

1166<p id="_717" tabindex="-1">

1167Ovarian, fallopian tube, or peritoneal cancer may not cause early signs or symptoms. When signs or symptoms do appear, the cancer is often advanced. Signs and symptoms include the following:</p>

1168<div class="pdq-content-list">

1169<ul id="_718">

1170<li>

1171Pain, swelling, or a feeling of pressure in the abdomen or pelvis.</li>

1172<li>

1173Urinary urgency or frequency.</li>

1174<li>

1175Difficulty eating or feeling full.</li>

1176<li>

1177A lump in the pelvic area.</li>

1178<li>

1179Gastrointestinal problems such as gas, bloating, or constipation.</li>

1180</ul>

1181</div>

1182<p id="_719" tabindex="-1">

1183 These symptoms often go unrecognized, leading to delays in diagnosis. Efforts have been made to enhance physician and patient awareness of the occurrence of these nonspecific symptoms.[<a href="#cit/section_1.33">

118433</a>

1185-<a href="#cit/section_1.37">

118637</a>

1187] </p>

1188<p id="_825" tabindex="-1">

1189Screening procedures such as gynecologic assessment, vaginal ultrasound, and cancer antigen 125 (CA-125) assay have had low predictive value in detecting ovarian cancer in women without special risk factors.[<a href="#cit/section_1.38">

119038</a>

1191,<a href="#cit/section_1.39">

119239</a>

1193] As a result of these confounding factors, annual mortality in ovarian cancer is approximately 65% of the incidence rate.</p>

1194<p id="_5" tabindex="-1">

1195Most patients with ovarian cancer have widespread disease at presentation. Early peritoneal spread of the most common subtype of high-grade serous cancers may relate to serous cancers starting in the fimbriae of the fallopian tubes or in the peritoneum, readily explaining why such cancers are detected at an advanced stage. Conversely, high-grade serous cancers are underrepresented among stage I cancers of the ovary. Other types of ovarian cancers are, in fact, overrepresented in cancers detected in stages I and II. This type of ovarian cancer usually spreads via local shedding into the peritoneal cavity

1196followed by implantation on the peritoneum and via local invasion of bowel and

1197bladder. The incidence of positive nodes at primary surgery has been reported

1198to be as high as 24% in patients with stage I disease, 50% in patients with stage II

1199disease, 74% in patients with stage III disease, and 73% in patients with stage

1200IV disease. The pelvic nodes were involved as often as the

1201para-aortic nodes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.40">

120240</a>

1203] Tumor cells may also block diaphragmatic lymphatics. The

1204resulting impairment of lymphatic drainage of the peritoneum is thought to play

1205a role in development of ascites in ovarian cancer. Transdiaphragmatic

1206spread to the pleura is common.

1207</p>

1208</section>

1209<section id="_676">

1210<h3 id="_676_toc">

1211Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</h3>

1212<p id="_720" tabindex="-1">

1213The following tests and procedures may be used in the diagnosis and staging of ovarian epithelial, fallopian tube, or primary peritoneal cancer:</p>

1214<div class="pdq-content-list">

1215<ul id="_721">

1216<li>

1217Physical exam and history.</li>

1218<li>

1219Pelvic exam.</li>

1220<li>

1221CA-125 assay.</li>

1222<li>

1223Ultrasonography (pelvic or transvaginal).</li>

1224<li>

1225Computed tomography (CT) scan.</li>

1226<li>

1227Positron emission tomography (PET) scan.</li>

1228<li>

1229Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).</li>

1230<li>

1231Chest x-ray.</li>

1232<li>

1233Biopsy.</li>

1234</ul>

1235</div>

1236<p id="_947" tabindex="-1">

1237CA-125 levels can be elevated in other malignancies and benign gynecologic problems such as endometriosis. CA-125 levels and histology are used to diagnose epithelial ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_1.41">

123841</a>

1239,<a href="#cit/section_1.42">

124042</a>

1241]</p>

1242</section>

1243<section id="_561">

1244<h3 id="_561_toc">

1245Prognostic Factors</h3>

1246<p id="_722" tabindex="-1">

1247Prognosis for patients with ovarian cancer is influenced by multiple factors. Multivariate

1248analyses suggest that the most important favorable prognostic factors include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_1.43">

124943</a>

1250-<a href="#cit/section_1.47">

125147</a>

1252]</p>

1253<div class="pdq-content-list">

1254<ul id="_723">

1255<li>

1256 Younger age.</li>

1257<li>

1258Good performance status.</li>

1259<li>

1260Cell type other than mucinous or clear cell.</li>

1261<li>

1262Well-differentiated tumor.</li>

1263<li>

1264Early-stage disease.</li>

1265<li>

1266Absence of ascites.</li>

1267<li>

1268Lower disease volume before surgical

1269debulking.</li>

1270<li>

1271Smaller residual tumor after primary

1272cytoreductive surgery.</li>

1273<li>

1274<em class="gene-name">

1275BRCA1</em>

1276 or <em class="gene-name">

1277BRCA2</em>

1278 mutation carrier.</li>

1279</ul>

1280</div>

1281<p id="_578" tabindex="-1">

1282For patients with stage I disease, the most

1283important prognostic factor associated with relapse is grade, followed by dense adherence and

1284large-volume ascites.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

128548</a>

1286] Stage I tumors have a high proportion of low-grade serous cancers. These cancers have a derivation distinctly different from that of high-grade serous cancers, which usually present in stages III and IV. Many high-grade serous cancers originate in the fallopian tube and other areas of extrauterine M&#252;llerian epithelial origin.</p>

1287<p id="_997" tabindex="-1">

1288If the tumor is grade 3, densely adherent, or stage IC, the chance of relapse and death from ovarian cancer is as much as 30%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.48">

128948</a>

1290-<a href="#cit/section_1.51">

129151</a>

1292]</p>

1293<p id="_826" tabindex="-1">

1294The use of DNA flow cytometric analysis of tumors from patients with stage I and stage

1295IIA disease may identify those at high risk.[<a href="#cit/section_1.52">

129652</a>

1297] Patients with

1298clear cell histology appear to have a worse prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.53">

129953</a>

1300] Patients with a

1301significant component of transitional cell carcinoma appear to have a better

1302prognosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.54">

130354</a>

1304]</p>

1305<p id="_532" tabindex="-1">

1306Case-control studies suggest that <em class="gene-name">

1307BRCA1</em>

1308 and <em class="gene-name">

1309BRCA2</em>

1310 mutation carriers have improved responses to chemotherapy when compared with patients with sporadic epithelial ovarian cancer. This may be the result of a deficient homologous DNA repair mechanism in these tumors, which leads to increased sensitivity to chemotherapy agents.[<a href="#cit/section_1.55">

131155</a>

1312,<a href="#cit/section_1.56">

131356</a>

1314] </p>

1315</section>

1316<section id="_708">

1317<h3 id="_708_toc">

1318Follow-up</h3>

1319<p id="_1002" tabindex="-1">

1320Because of the low specificity and sensitivity of the CA-125 assay, serial CA-125 monitoring of patients undergoing treatment for recurrence may be useful. However, whether that confers a net benefit has not yet been determined. There is little guidance about patient follow-up after initial induction therapy. Neither early detection by imaging nor by CA-125 elevation has been shown to alter outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_1.57">

132157</a>

1322] For more information, see the <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_82">

1323Treatment of Recurrent or Persistent Ovarian Epithelial, FTC, and PPC</a>

1324 section.</p>

1325</section>

1566 <h2>

1567Cellular Classification of Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h2>

1568

1569 <div id="_section_9" class="pdq-sections">

1570<p id="_726" tabindex="-1">

1571<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_998">

1572Table 1</a>

1573 describes the histological classification of ovarian epithelial cancer, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC).

1669<tr>

1670<td>

1671Other malignant tumors (malignant tumors other than those of the common epithelial types are not to be included with the categories listed above)</td>

1672<td>

1673&#160;</td>

1674</tr>

1675</tbody>

1676</table>

1677</div>

1678

1679 </section>

1680

1681

1682

1683 <section id="_13">

1684

1685 <h2>

1686Stage Information for Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h2>

1687

1688 <div id="_section_13" class="pdq-sections">

1689<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1690<h6>

1691In This Section</h6>

1692<ul>

1693<li>

1694<a href="#_359_toc">

1695The F&#233;d&#233;ration Internationale de Gyn&#233;cologie et d&#8217;Obst&#233;trique (FIGO) Staging</a>

1696</li>

1697</ul>

1698</nav>

1699<p id="_811" tabindex="-1">

1700In the absence of extra-abdominal metastatic disease, definitive staging of

1701ovarian cancer requires surgery. The role of surgery in patients with stage

1702IV ovarian cancer and extra-abdominal disease is yet to be established. If disease

1703appears to be limited to the ovaries or pelvis, it is essential at laparotomy

1704to obtain peritoneal washings and to examine and biopsy or obtain cytological brushings of the following:

1705</p>

1706<div class="pdq-content-list">

1707<ul id="_830">

1708<li>

1709Diaphragm.</li>

1710<li>

1711Both paracolic gutters.</li>

1712<li>

1713Pelvic peritoneum.</li>

1714<li>

1715Para-aortic and pelvic nodes.</li>

1716<li>

1717Infracolic omentum.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

17181</a>

1719]</li>

1720</ul>

1721</div>

1722<section id="_359">

1723<h3 id="_359_toc">

1724The F&#233;d&#233;ration Internationale de Gyn&#233;cologie et d&#8217;Obst&#233;trique (FIGO) Staging</h3>

1725<p id="_812" tabindex="-1">

1726The FIGO and the American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) have designated staging to define ovarian epithelial cancer. The FIGO-approved staging system for ovarian epithelial cancer, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC) is the one most commonly used.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

17272</a>

1728,<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

17293</a>

1730]</p>

2062 <section id="_677">

2063

2064 <h2>

2065Treatment Option Overview</h2>

2066

2067 <div id="_section_677" class="pdq-sections">

2068<p id="_728" tabindex="-1">

2069Treatment options for patients with all stages of ovarian epithelial cancer, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC) have consisted of surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy. </p>

2070<p id="_831" tabindex="-1">

2071Early stage refers to stages I and II. However, because of high recurrence rates for stage II patients in early-stage disease trials, patients with stage II cancers have been included with patients who have more advanced-stage cancer in Gynecologic Oncology Group clinical trials since 2009. Going forward, stage I will remain a separate category for treatment considerations, but high-grade serous stage II cancers are likely to be included with more advanced stages.</p>

2072<p id="_729" tabindex="-1">

2073Numerous clinical trials are in progress to

2074refine existing therapies and test the value of different approaches to

2075postoperative drug and radiation therapy. Patients with any stage of ovarian

2076cancer are appropriate candidates for clinical trials.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

20771</a>

2078,<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

20792</a>

2080] Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

2081NCI website</a>

2082.</p>

2083<p id="_730" tabindex="-1">

2084The treatment options for ovarian epithelial cancer, FTC, and PPC are presented in <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_816">

2085Table 6</a>

2086.</p>

2199 <section id="_40">

2200

2201 <h2>

2202Treatment of Early-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h2>

2203

2204 <div id="_section_40" class="pdq-sections">

2205<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2206<h6>

2207In This Section</h6>

2208<ul>

2209<li>

2210<a href="#_678_toc">

2211Treatment Options for Early-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC<ul>

2212<li>

2213<a href="#_680_toc">

2214Surgery with or without chemotherapy</a>

2215</li>

2216</ul>

2217</a>

2218</li>

2219<li>

2220<a href="#_TrialSearch_40_sid_5_toc">

2221Current Clinical Trials</a>

2222</li>

2223</ul>

2224</nav>

2225<p id="_731" tabindex="-1">

2226 Early stage refers to stage I and stage II. However, because of high recurrence rates for stage II patients in early-stage disease trials, patients with stage II cancers have been included with patients who have more advanced-stage cancer in Gynecologic Oncology Group (GOG) clinical trials since 2009. Going forward, stage I will remain a separate category for treatment considerations, but high-grade serous stage II cancers are likely to be included with more advanced stages.</p>

2227<section id="_678">

2228<h3 id="_678_toc">

2229Treatment Options for Early-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h3>

2230<p id="_695" tabindex="-1">

2231Treatment options for early-stage ovarian epithelial, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC) include the following:</p>

2232<div class="pdq-content-list">

2233<ol id="_696">

2234<li>

2235<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_680">

2236Surgery with or without chemotherapy.</a>

2237</li>

2238</ol>

2239</div>

2240<section id="_680">

2241<h4 id="_680_toc">

2242Surgery with or without chemotherapy</h4>

2243<p id="_732" tabindex="-1">

2244If the tumor is well differentiated or moderately well differentiated, surgery alone may be adequate treatment for patients with stage IA or IB disease. Surgery includes

2245hysterectomy, bilateral salpingo-oophorectomy, and omentectomy. The undersurface of the diaphragm

2246is visualized and biopsied. Biopsies of the pelvic and abdominal peritoneum and

2247the pelvic and para-aortic lymph nodes are also performed. Peritoneal washings are routinely obtained.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

22481</a>

2249,<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

22502</a>

2251] In patients who desire childbearing

2252and have grade 1 tumors, unilateral salpingo-oophorectomy may be

2253associated with a low risk of recurrence.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

22543</a>

2255]</p>

2256<p id="_733" tabindex="-1">

2257In the United States, except for the most favorable subset of patients (those with stage IA well-differentiated disease), evidence based on double-blinded, randomized, controlled trials with total mortality end points supports adjuvant treatment with cisplatin, carboplatin, and paclitaxel.</p>

2258<p id="_881" tabindex="-1">

2259Evidence (surgery with or without chemotherapy):</p>

2260<div class="pdq-content-list">

2261<ol id="_882">

2262<li>

2263In two large European trials, the European Organisation for Research and Treatment of Cancer-Adjuvant ChemoTherapy in Ovarian Neoplasm trial (EORTC-ACTION) and International Collaborative Ovarian Neoplasm trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00002477">

2264MRC-ICON1</a>

2265 [NCT00002477]), patients with stage IA (grade 2) and stage IB (grade 3), all stage IC and stage II ovarian epithelial, and all stage I and stage IIA clear cell carcinoma were randomly assigned to receive adjuvant chemotherapy or observation.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

22664</a>

2267-<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

22686</a>

2269]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2270<ol id="_883" class="lower-alpha">

2271<li>

2272The EORTC-ACTION trial required at least four cycles of carboplatin or cisplatin-based chemotherapy as treatment. Although surgical staging criteria were monitored, inadequate staging was not an exclusion criterion.[<a href="#cit/section_5.4">

22734</a>

2274]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2275<ul id="_884">

2276<li>

2277Recurrence-free survival (RFS) was improved for patients in the adjuvant chemotherapy arm (hazard ratio [HR], 0.63; <em>

2278P</em>

2279 = .02), but overall survival (OS) was not affected (HR, 0.69; 95% confidence interval [CI], 0.44&#8211;1.08; <em>

2280P</em>

2281 = .10).</li>

2282<li>

2283OS was improved by chemotherapy in the subset of patients with inadequate surgical staging.</li>

2284</ul>

2285</div>

2286</li>

2287<li>

2288The MRC-ICON1 trial randomly assigned patients to receive six cycles of single-agent carboplatin or cisplatin or platinum-based chemotherapy (usually cyclophosphamide, doxorubicin, and cisplatin) versus observation and had entry criteria similar to the EORTC-ACTION trial; however, the MRC-ICON1 trial did not monitor whether adequate surgical staging was performed.[<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

22895</a>

2290] When the results of the trials were combined, the difference in OS achieved statistical significance.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2291<ul id="_885">

2292<li>

2293Both RFS and OS were significantly improved; the 5-year survival rates were 79% for patients who received adjuvant chemotherapy versus 70% for those who did not receive adjuvant chemotherapy.</li>

2294</ul>

2295</div>

2296</li>

2297<li>

2298An analysis of pooled data from both studies demonstrated the following:[<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

22996</a>

2300][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2301Level of evidence A1</a>

2302]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2303<ul id="_886">

2304<li>

2305Patients who received chemotherapy showed significant improvement in RFS (HR, 0.64; 95% CI, 0.50&#8211;0.82; <em>

2306P</em>

2307 = .001) and OS (HR, 0.67; 95% CI, 0.50&#8211;0.90; <em>

2308P</em>

2309 = .008). The 5-year OS rate was 82% for patients who received chemotherapy and 74% for patients who underwent observation (difference, 8%; 95% CI, 2%&#8211;12%).[<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

23106</a>

2311][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2312Level of evidence A1</a>

2313]</li>

2314<li>

2315An accompanying editorial emphasized that the focus of subsequent trials must be to identify patients who do not require additional therapy among the early ovarian cancer subset.[<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

23167</a>

2317] Optimal staging is one way to better identify these patients.</li>

2318</ul>

2319</div>

2320</li>

2321</ol>

2322</div>

2323</li>

2324<li>

2325The GOG-0157 trial evaluated whether six cycles of chemotherapy were superior to three cycles for patients with early-stage, high-risk epithelial ovarian cancer after primary surgery. Eligible patients were those with stage IA grade 3 or clear cell histology, stage IB grade 3 or clear cell histology, all stage IC, and all stage II. Patients were randomly assigned to receive either three or six cycles of the combination of paclitaxel (175 mg/m<span class="sup">

23262</span>

2327 administered over 3 hours) and carboplatin dosed (area under the curve, 7.5) over 30 minutes and given every 21 days. The primary end point was RFS, and the study was powered to detect a 50% decrease in the recurrence rate at 5 years. A total of 427 patients were eligible.[<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

23288</a>

2329]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2330<ul id="_887">

2331<li>

2332No significant difference in cumulative incidence of recurrence was found when three cycles (25.4%) were compared with six cycles (20.1%) (HR, 0.76; 95% CI, 0.5&#8211;1.13) or OS for three cycles (81%) versus six cycles (83%) (HR, 1.02; <em>

2333P</em>

2334 = .94).[<a href="#cit/section_5.8">

23358</a>

2336][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2337Level of evidence B1</a>

2338]</li>

2339<li>

2340As expected, the use of six cycles was associated with increased grade 3 or 4 neurological toxic effects and increased grade 4 hematologic toxic effects.</li>

2341<li>

2342Although surgical staging was required for study entry, an audit revealed that 29% of the patients had either incomplete documentation of their surgery or insufficient surgical effort.</li>

2343<li>

2344In a post hoc analysis of the patients who underwent complete surgical staging, three additional cycles of chemotherapy decreased the risk of recurrence by only 3%. The cumulative incidence of recurrence within 5 years was 18% for women with stage I disease and 33% for women with stage II disease.</li>

2345</ul>

2346</div>

2347<p id="_888" tabindex="-1">

2348Given the increased risk of recurrence in patients with stage II disease and in those classified as having high-grade serous cancer, the GOG after 2007 opted to include patients with stage II disease in advanced ovarian cancer trials (for more information, see the <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_61">

2349Treatment of Advanced-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</a>

2350 section). Although the routine use of six cycles of chemotherapy is promulgated by guidelines, on subset analyses it is a source of controversy. Platinum-based chemotherapy including paclitaxel for three or six cycles has been evaluated by the GOG in additional trials that included prolonged maintenance paclitaxel, before phasing out early-stage clinical trials.</p>

2351</li>

2352<li>

2353Patients with stage II ovarian cancer were enrolled in a Japanese Gynecology Oncology Group study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00226915">

2354JGOG-3016</a>

2355 [NCT00226915]) that tested a weekly dosing schedule versus the conventional every-3-week dosing schedule in first-line ovarian cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.9">

23569</a>

2357-<a href="#cit/section_5.11">

235811</a>

2359]</li>

2360</ol>

2361</div>

2362<p id="_839" tabindex="-1">

2363The following treatments have been largely displaced by the adoption of carboplatin plus paclitaxel for early stages of high-grade ovarian cancers:</p>

2364<div class="pdq-content-list">

2365<ul id="_734">

2366<li>

2367Intraperitoneal phosphorus P 32 or radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

23681</a>

2369,<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

237012</a>

2371,<a href="#cit/section_5.13">

237213</a>

2373]

2374</li>

2375<li>

2376Platinum-based systemic chemotherapy alone or in combination with alkylating agents.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

23771</a>

2378,<a href="#cit/section_5.12">

237912</a>

2380,<a href="#cit/section_5.14">

238114</a>

2382-<a href="#cit/section_5.16">

238316</a>

2384]

2385</li>

2386</ul>

2387</div>

2388</section>

2474 <section id="_61">

2475

2476 <h2>

2477Treatment of Advanced-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h2>

2478

2479 <div id="_section_61" class="pdq-sections">

2480<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2481<h6>

2482In This Section</h6>

2483<ul>

2484<li>

2485<a href="#_682_toc">

2486Treatment Options for Advanced-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC<ul>

2487<li>

2488<a href="#_684_toc">

2489Surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy</a>

2490</li>

2491<li>

2492<a href="#_595_toc">

2493Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and/or additional consolidation therapy</a>

2494</li>

2495<li>

2496<a href="#_692_toc">

2497Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and the addition of bevacizumab to induction and/or consolidation therapy</a>

2498</li>

2499<li>

2500<a href="#_1123_toc">

2501Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and the addition of poly (ADP-ribose) polymerase (PARP) inhibitors to induction and/or consolidation therapy</a>

2502</li>

2503<li>

2504<a href="#_817_toc">

2505Other consolidation and/or maintenance therapy trials</a>

2506</li>

2507<li>

2508<a href="#_1474_toc">

2509Treatment options under clinical evaluation</a>

2510</li>

2511</ul>

2512</a>

2513</li>

2514<li>

2515<a href="#_TrialSearch_61_sid_6_toc">

2516Current Clinical Trials</a>

2517</li>

2518</ul>

2519</nav>

2520<p id="_287" tabindex="-1">

2521Treatment options for patients with all stages of ovarian epithelial cancer, fallopian tube cancer (FTC), and primary peritoneal cancer (PPC) have consisted of surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy. Because of high recurrence rates for stage II patients in early-stage disease trials, patients with stage II cancers have been included with patients who have more advanced-stage cancer in Gynecologic Oncology Group (GOG) clinical trials since 2009. Going forward, stage I will remain a separate category for treatment considerations, but high-grade serous stage II cancers are likely to be included with more advanced stages.</p>

2522<p id="_1409" tabindex="-1">

2523The most common approach to advanced ovarian cancer is surgery followed by adjuvant platinum-based chemotherapy. Published trials, most with primary end points of progression-free survival (PFS), are listed in <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_673">

2524Table 7</a>

2525. A PFS end point was endorsed by the Gynecologic Cancer InterGroup (GCIC), but subsequently it was questioned in a systematic review and meta-analysis conducted by the GCIC.[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

25261</a>

2527] After a MEDLINE search of randomized clinical trials of newly-diagnosed patients with ovarian epithelial cancer, FTC, or PPC, all studies with a minimum sample of 60 patients published from 2001 through 2016 were used to extract PFS and overall survival (OS) at an individual level. The PFS was mostly based on measurement of CA-125 levels confirmed by radiological examination or by GCIC criteria. Of 17 trials that were individually assessed, five tested the addition of maintenance therapy, seven tested additional induction drugs, and five tested intensification therapy. No poly (ADP-ribose) polymerase (PARP) inhibitor trials were included in this meta-analysis. The analysis concluded that PFS is not an adequate surrogate for OS, but it was limited by the narrow range of treatment effects observed and by poststudy treatments. </p>

2528<section id="_682">

2529<h3 id="_682_toc">

2530Treatment Options for Advanced-Stage Ovarian Epithelial Cancer, FTC, and PPC</h3>

2531<p id="_706" tabindex="-1">

2532Treatment options for advanced-stage ovarian epithelial cancer, FTC, and PPC include the following:</p>

2533<div class="pdq-content-list">

2534<ol id="_707">

2535<li>

2536<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_684">

2537Surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy.</a>

2538</li>

2539<li>

2540<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_595">

2541Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and/or additional consolidation therapy</a>

2542.</li>

2543<li>

2544<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_692">

2545Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and the addition of bevacizumab to induction therapy and/or consolidation therapy</a>

2546.</li>

2547<li>

2548<a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_1123">

2549Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and the addition of PARP inhibitors to induction therapy and/or consolidation therapy</a>

2550.</li>

2551<li>

2552Chemotherapy for patients who cannot have surgery (although the impact on OS has not been proven).</li>

2553</ol>

2554</div>

2555<p id="_970" tabindex="-1">

2556Platinum-based chemotherapy is the initial treatment for all patients diagnosed with advanced disease who undergo surgical resection and are staged with cancer that has spread to the pelvic peritoneum (stage II) and beyond (stages III and IV). The role of surgery for patients with stage IV disease is unclear, but in most instances, the bulk of the disease is intra-abdominal, and surgical procedures similar to those used in the management of patients with stage II and III disease are applied. </p>

2557<p id="_739" tabindex="-1">

2558Surgery has historically been done by open laparotomy performed by gynecologic oncology surgeons, and has included hysterectomy, bilateral

2559salpingo-oophorectomy, omentectomy, and debulking of peritoneal implants (often including resection of the bowel or adjacent organs as needed) to reduce tumor to microscopic, if it can safely be performed.

2560</p>

2561<p id="_840" tabindex="-1">

2562The volume of disease left at the completion of the primary surgical procedure

2563in GOG studies has been related to patient survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

25642</a>

2565-<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

25665</a>

2567] A literature review showed that patients

2568with optimal cytoreduction had a median survival of 39 months compared with

2569survival of only 17 months in patients with suboptimal residual disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.2">

25702</a>

2571][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2572Level of evidence C1</a>

2573]</p>

2574<p id="_740" tabindex="-1">

2575However, in an analysis of 2,655 of the 4,312 patients enrolled in the largest GOG study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00011986">

2576GOG-0182</a>

2577 [NCT00011986]), only cytoreduction to nonvisible disease that is R0 (i.e., complete surgical resection) had an independent effect on survival. For more information, see the <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_684">

2578Surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy</a>

2579 section.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

25806</a>

2581] The GOG had conducted separate trials to establish a role for intraperitoneal (IP) therapy for women whose disease has been optimally cytoreduced (defined as &#8804;1 cm residuum) and for those who had suboptimal cytoreductions (&gt;1 cm residuum). For more information, see the <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_595">

2582Surgery before or after platinum-based chemotherapy and/or additional consolidation therapy</a>

2583 section.</p>

2584<p id="_725" tabindex="-1">

2585Suboptimally debulked stage III and stage IV patients

2586have inferior 5-year survival rates, but the gap has narrowed in trials that included taxanes and other drugs added to platinums.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

25877</a>

2588] By contrast, optimally debulked stage III patients treated with a combination of intravenous (IV) taxane and IP platinum plus taxane achieved a median survival of 66 months in a GOG trial.[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

25898</a>

2590][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2591Level of evidence A1</a>

2592] </p>

2593<section id="_684">

2594<h4 id="_684_toc">

2595Surgery followed by platinum-based chemotherapy</h4>

2596<p id="_745" tabindex="-1">

2597Platinum agents, such as cisplatin or its less-toxic second-generation analog, carboplatin, given either alone or in combination with other drugs, are the foundation of chemotherapy regimens used. Trials by various cooperative groups (conducted from 1999 to 2010) addressed issues of optimal dose intensity [<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

25989</a>

2599-<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

260011</a>

2601] for both cisplatin and carboplatin,[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

260212</a>

2603] schedule,[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

260413</a>

2605] and the equivalent results obtained with either of these platinum drugs, usually in combination with cyclophosphamide.[<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

260614</a>

2607] </p>

2608<p id="_841" tabindex="-1">

2609With the introduction of the taxane paclitaxel, two trials confirmed the superiority of cisplatin combined with paclitaxel when compared with the previous standard treatment of cisplatin plus cyclophosphamide.[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

261015</a>

2611,<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

261216</a>

2613] However, two trials that compared single-agent paclitaxel with either cisplatin or carboplatin (ICON3 and GOG-132) failed to confirm such superiority in all outcome parameters (i.e., response, time-to-progression, and survival) (see <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_673">

2614Table 7</a>

2615 for a list of these studies).</p>

2616<p id="_746" tabindex="-1">

2617Based on the evidence, the initial standard treatment for patients with ovarian cancer is the combination of cisplatin or carboplatin with paclitaxel (defined as induction chemotherapy).</p>

2618<p id="_891" tabindex="-1">

2619Evidence (combination of cisplatin or carboplatin with paclitaxel):</p>

2620<div class="pdq-content-list">

2621<ol id="_892">

2622<li>

2623GOG-132 was widely regarded as showing that sequential treatment with cisplatin and paclitaxel was equivalent to the combination of cisplatin-plus-paclitaxel; however, many patients crossed over before disease progression. Moreover, the cisplatin-only arm was more toxic than the combination of cisplatin (75 mg/m<span class="sup">

26242</span>

2625) and paclitaxel because it utilized a 100 mg/m<span class="sup">

26262</span>

2627 cisplatin dose per cycle.[<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

262817</a>

2629]</li>

2630<li>

2631The Medical Research Council study (MRC-ICON3) compared carboplatin monotherapy with the combination of carboplatin and paclitaxel. While MRC-ICON3 had fewer early crossovers than GOG-132, it yielded similar outcomes for carboplatin monotherapy, including OS (albeit with less toxicity) compared with the combination treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

263218</a>

2633]</li>

2634</ol>

2635</div>

2636<p id="_748" tabindex="-1">

2637Since the adoption of the standard combination of platinum plus taxane nearly worldwide, clinical trials have demonstrated the following:</p>

2638<div class="pdq-content-list">

2639<ol id="_845">

2640<li>

2641Noninferiority of carboplatin plus paclitaxel versus cisplatin plus paclitaxel.[<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

264215</a>

2643,<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

264416</a>

2645,<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

264619</a>

2647]</li>

2648<li>

2649Noninferiority of carboplatin plus paclitaxel versus carboplatin plus docetaxel.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

265020</a>

2651]</li>

2652<li>

2653No

2654advantage but increased toxic effects of adding epirubicin to the carboplatin plus paclitaxel doublet.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

265521</a>

2656]</li>

2657<li>

2658Noninferiority of carboplatin plus paclitaxel versus sequential carboplatin-containing doublets with either gemcitabine or topotecan; or, triplets with the addition of gemcitabine or pegylated liposomal doxorubicin to the reference doublet as shown below:[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

265922</a>

2660,<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

266123</a>

2662]<div class="pdq-content-list">

2663<ol id="_846" class="lower-alpha">

2664<li>

2665From February 2001 to September 2004, 4,312 women with stage III or stage IV ovarian epithelial cancer, FTC, or PPC participating in the <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00011986">

2666GOG-0182</a>

2667 trial were randomly assigned to four different experimental arms or to a reference treatment consisting of carboplatin (area under the curve [AUC], 6) and paclitaxel (175 mg/m<span class="sup">

26682</span>

2669) every 3 weeks for eight cycles.[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

267022</a>

2671] Stratification factors were residual-disease status and the intention to perform interval debulking surgery. <div class="pdq-content-list">

2672<ul id="_847">

2673<li>

2674None of the experimental regimens was inferior.</li>

2675<li>

2676Lethal events attributable to treatment occurred in less than 1% of patients without clustering to any one regimen.</li>

2677<li>

2678With a median follow-up of 3.7 years, the adjusted relative risk of death ranged from 0.952 to 1.114, with the control arm achieving a PFS of 16.0 months and a median OS of 44.1 months.</li>

2679</ul>

2680</div>

2681 <p id="_1399" tabindex="-1">

2682Moreover, for the stage III patients who made up 84% to 87% of patients, PFS differences were only noted if surgery achieved R0 resections:[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

268322</a>

2684]</p>

2685<div class="pdq-content-list">

2686<ul id="_949">

2687<li>

2688PFS in patients with residuum larger than 1 cm was 13 months, and OS was 33 months.</li>

2689<li>

2690With residuum 1 cm or smaller, PFS was 16 months, and OS was 40 months.</li>

2691<li>

2692With R0 resection (e.g., no residuum or microscopic residuum only), PFS was 29 months, and OS was 68 months.</li>

2693</ul>

2694</div>

2695</li>

2696</ol>

2697</div>

2698</li>

2699</ol>

2700</div>

2701<p id="_1003" tabindex="-1">

2702In gynecologic cancer, as opposed to breast cancer, weekly paclitaxel was not explored in phase III trials before 2004. The positive results from the <a href="/types/ovarian/hp/ovarian-epithelial-treatment-pdq#_849">

2703Japanese Gynecologic Oncology Group (JGOG) 3016 study</a>

2704 subsequently led to early adoption of divided-dose paclitaxel as the standard treatment, but with only partial confirmation of its superior results.</p>

2705<p id="_848" tabindex="-1">

2706Evidence (dose-dense [weekly] treatment schedule):</p>

2707<div class="pdq-content-list">

2708<ol id="_849">

2709<li>

2710A JGOG trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00226915">

2711JGOG-3016</a>

2712 [NCT00226915]) accrued 637 patients and randomly assigned them to six to nine cycles of weekly (dose-dense) paclitaxel (80 mg/m<span class="sup">

27132</span>

2714) or to the standard every-21-day schedule of paclitaxel at 180 mg/m<span class="sup">

27152</span>

2716. Both regimens were given with carboplatin (AUC, 6) in every-3-week cycles. The primary study end point was PFS with a goal of detecting a PFS increase from 16 months to 21 months in patients receiving the weekly paclitaxel-based regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

271724</a>

2718,<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

271925</a>

2720] Although more toxic, the weekly paclitaxel regimen did not adversely affect quality of life when compared with the intermittent schedule.[<a href="#cit/section_6.26">

272126</a>

2722][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2723Level of evidence B1</a>

2724]<p id="_850" tabindex="-1">

2725Other than ethnicity, this trial population may have differed from GOG and other studies in that patients were younger (average age, 57 years). Twenty percent of patients had stage II disease and 33% of patients had histologies other than high-grade serous or endometrioid cancer. Also, 11% of patients were entered while receiving neoadjuvant treatment, which was an all-inclusive way of assessing treatments other than chemotherapy in first-line settings. The JGOG-3016 study results demonstrated the following:</p>

2726<div class="pdq-content-list">

2727<ul id="_853">

2728<li>

2729At the 1.5-year follow-up after cessation of treatment, patients who received the weekly regimen had a median PFS of 28.0 months (95% confidence interval [CI], 22.3&#8211;35.4), and patients who received the intermittent regimen had a median PFS of 17.2 months (range, 15.7&#8211;21.1; hazard ratio [HR], 0.71), favoring the weekly regimen (<em>

2730P</em>

2731 = .0015).</li>

2732<li>

2733A 2013 update revealed an increase in median survival for patients who received the weekly regimen (median OS, 8.3 years vs. 5.1 years; <em>

2734P</em>

2735 = .040); the intermittent regimen results are also noteworthy relative to other clinical trials of weekly dosing schedules. </li>

2736</ul>

2737</div>

2738</li>

2739<li>

2740In a phase III trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00660842">

2741MITO-7</a>

2742 [NCT00660842]), the outcomes of 406 patients assigned to weekly paclitaxel (60 mg/m<span class="sup">

27432</span>

2744) administered with weekly carboplatin (AUC, 2) were compared with those of 404 patients receiving the conventional every-3-week regimen of paclitaxel and carboplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_6.27">

274527</a>

2746][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810017&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2747Level of evidence A1</a>

2748] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2749<ul id="_855">

2750<li>

2751The results failed to confirm the superiority of this weekly schedule (18.3 months PFS for the weekly arm vs. 17.3 months PFS for the standard arm [HR, 0.96; 95% CI, 0.80&#8211;1.16]).</li>

2752<li>

2753The treatments did not differ in toxic effects. A decrease in quality of life (assessed by the Functional Assessment of Cancer Therapy Ovarian Trial Outcome Index questionnaire) was not seen in the weekly arm compared with the every-3-week arm.</li>

2754</ul>

2755</div>

2756 </li>

2757<li>

2758 <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01167712">

2759GOG-0262</a>

2760 (NCT01167712) is a phase III study that compared weekly paclitaxel (80 mg/m<span class="sup">

27612</span>

2762) to every-3-week dosing (175 mg/m<span class="sup">

27632</span>

2764), both with the conventional every-3-week carboplatin (AUC, 6) regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.28">

276528</a>

2766][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

2767Level of evidence B1</a>

2768] An option to give bevacizumab every 3 weeks beginning with cycle two and continuing until cycle six and followed by bevacizumab alone for 1 year, as in GOG-0218, was included for both arms. This option was applied in about 84% of all patients.<div class="pdq-content-list">

2769<ul id="_1021">

2770<li>

2771 Overall, the weekly paclitaxel regimen failed to prolong PFS compared with the every-3-week regimen (14.7 months vs. 14.0 months), with an HR for progression or death of 0.89 (95% CI, 0.74&#8211;1.06). </li>

2772<li>

2773 However, among patients not receiving bevacizumab, the weekly paclitaxel arm had significantly prolonged PFS (14.2 months vs. 10.3 months), with an HR of 0.62 (95% CI, 0.40&#8211;0.95; <em>

2774P</em>

2775 = .03)</li>

2776<li>

2777The weekly paclitaxel regimen had a higher rate of grade 3 or 4 anemia (36% vs. 16%) and grade 2 to 4 sensory neuropathy (26% vs. 18%).</li>

2778</ul>

2779</div>

2780</li>

2781<li>

2782<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT01654146">

2783ICON8</a>

2784 (NCT01654146) is another phase III trial that compared weekly paclitaxel with every-3-week dosing, with another arm that compared weekly paclitaxel with weekly carboplatin (AUC, 2 &#739; 6 cycles).[<a href="#cit/section_6.29">

278529</a>

2786] <div class="pdq-content-list">

2787<ul id="_1431">

2788<li>

2789This large study did not demonstrate any significant differences between the arms. </li>

2790<li>

2791A separate quality-of-life study found no difference in global quality of life among the three groups at a 9-month cross-sectional analysis, although the weekly paclitaxel schedules scored significantly lower in longitudinal analyses.[<a href="#cit/section_6.30">

279230</a>

2793]</li>

2794</ul>

2795</div>

2796 </li>

2797</ol>

2798</div>
